<div id="mw-indicator-pp-default" class="mw-indicator"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi" title="This article is semi-protected due to vandalism"><img alt="Page semi-protected" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Padlock-silver.svg/20px-Padlock-silver.svg.png" width="20" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Padlock-silver.svg/30px-Padlock-silver.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Padlock-silver.svg/40px-Padlock-silver.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></div>
</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Technology</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about the use and knowledge of techniques and processes for producing goods and services. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Technology_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Technology (disambiguation)">Technology (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg/300px-Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg" width="300" height="359" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg/450px-Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg/600px-Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1779" data-file-height="2126" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dampfturbine_Montage01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> with the case opened. Most electricity is produced by <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a> with turbines like this one. Electricity consumption and living standards are highly correlated.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Electrification is believed to be the most important engineering achievement of the 20th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Technology</b> ("science of craft", from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">τέχνη</span>, <i>techne</i>, "art, skill, cunning of hand"; and <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">-λογία</span>, <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-logia" class="extiw" title="wikt:-logia">-logia</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Liddell_1980_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liddell_1980-2">[2]</a></sup>) is the collection of <a href="/wiki/Art_techniques_and_materials" class="mw-redirect" title="Art techniques and materials">techniques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skill" title="Skill">skills</a>, <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/method" class="extiw" title="wikt:method">methods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Business_process" title="Business process">processes</a> used in the production of <a href="/wiki/Good_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Good (economics)">goods</a> or <a href="/wiki/Service_(economics)" title="Service (economics)">services</a> or in the accomplishment of objectives, such as <a href="/wiki/Scientific_investigation" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific investigation">scientific investigation</a>. Technology can be the <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> of techniques, processes, and the like, or it can be embedded in <a href="/wiki/Machines" class="mw-redirect" title="Machines">machines</a> which can be operated without detailed knowledge of their workings.</p>
<p>The human species' use of technology began with the conversion of <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> into simple tools. The <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistoric</a> discovery of <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">how to control fire</a> and the later <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> increased the available sources of food and the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a> helped humans to travel in and control their environment. Developments in historic times, including the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Telephone" title="Telephone">telephone</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, have lessened physical barriers to <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> and allowed humans to interact freely on a global scale. The steady progress of <a href="/wiki/Military_technology" title="Military technology">military technology</a> has brought <a href="/wiki/Weapon" title="Weapon">weapons</a> of ever-increasing destructive power, from <a href="/wiki/Club_(weapon)" title="Club (weapon)">clubs</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>.</p>
<p>Technology has many effects. It has helped develop more advanced <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">economies</a> (including today's <a href="/wiki/Economic_globalization" title="Economic globalization">global economy</a>) and has allowed the rise of a <a href="/wiki/Conspicuous_leisure" title="Conspicuous leisure">leisure class</a>. Many technological processes produce unwanted by-products known as <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> and deplete natural resources to the detriment of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a>. Various implementations of technology influence the <a href="/wiki/Value_(personal_and_cultural)" class="mw-redirect" title="Value (personal and cultural)">values</a> of a society and new technology often raises new ethical questions. Examples include the rise of the notion of <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">efficiency</a> in terms of human <a href="/wiki/Productivity" title="Productivity">productivity</a>, and the challenges of <a href="/wiki/Bioethics" title="Bioethics">bioethics</a>.</p>
<p>Philosophical debates have arisen over the use of technology, with disagreements over whether technology improves the <a href="/wiki/Human_condition" title="Human condition">human condition</a> or worsens it. <a href="/wiki/Neo-Luddism" title="Neo-Luddism">Neo-Luddism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-primitivism" title="Anarcho-primitivism">anarcho-primitivism</a>, and similar reactionary movements criticise the pervasiveness of technology in the modern world, arguing that it harms the environment and alienates people; proponents of ideologies such as <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Techno-progressivism" title="Techno-progressivism">techno-progressivism</a> view continued technological progress as beneficial to society and the <a href="/wiki/Human_condition" title="Human condition">human condition</a>.</p>
<p>Until recently, it was believed that the development of technology was restricted only to human beings, but 21st century scientific studies indicate that other <a href="/wiki/Primates" class="mw-redirect" title="Primates">primates</a> and certain <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphin</a> communities have developed simple tools and passed their knowledge to other generations.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_usage">Definition and usage</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg/170px-Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg/255px-Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg/340px-Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Handtiegelpresse_von_1811.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The spread of paper and printing to the West, as in this <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, helped <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politician" title="Politician">politicians</a> communicate their ideas easily, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>; an example of technology as cultural force.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The use of the term "technology" has changed significantly over the last 200 years. Before the 20th century, the term was uncommon in English, and usually referred to the description or study of the <a href="/wiki/Useful_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Useful arts">useful arts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The term was often connected to technical education, as in the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (chartered in 1861).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term "technology" rose to prominence in the 20th century in connection with the <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">Second Industrial Revolution</a>. The term's meanings changed in the early 20th century when American social scientists, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Thorstein_Veblen" title="Thorstein Veblen">Thorstein Veblen</a>, translated ideas from the German concept of <i><a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technik" class="extiw" title="de:Technik">Technik</a></i> into "technology." In German and other European languages, a distinction exists between <i>technik</i> and <i>technologie</i> that is absent in English, which usually translates both terms as "technology." By the 1930s, "technology" referred not only to the study of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_arts" title="Industrial arts">industrial arts</a> but to the industrial arts themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1937, the American sociologist Read Bain wrote that "technology includes all tools, machines, utensils, weapons, instruments, housing, clothing, communicating and transporting devices and the skills by which we produce and use them."<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Bain's definition remains common among scholars today, especially social scientists, but equally prominent is the definition of technology as applied science, especially among scientists and engineers, although most social scientists who study technology reject this definition.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> More recently, scholars have borrowed from European philosophers of "technique" to extend the meaning of technology to various forms of instrumental reason, as in <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Foucault</a>'s work on <a href="/wiki/Technologies_of_the_self" class="mw-redirect" title="Technologies of the self">technologies of the self</a> (<i>techniques de soi</i>).</p>
<p>Dictionaries and scholars have offered a variety of definitions. The <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster%27s_Advanced_Learner%27s_English_Dictionary" title="Merriam-Webster's Advanced Learner's English Dictionary">Merriam-Webster Learner's Dictionary</a></i> offers a definition of the term: "the use of science in industry, engineering, etc., to invent useful things or to solve problems" and "a machine, piece of equipment, method, etc., that is created by technology."<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ursula_Franklin" title="Ursula Franklin">Ursula Franklin</a>, in her 1989 "Real World of Technology" lecture, gave another definition of the concept; it is "practice, the way we do things around here."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The term is often used to imply a specific field of technology, or to refer to <a href="/wiki/High_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="High technology">high technology</a> or just <a href="/wiki/Consumer_electronics" title="Consumer electronics">consumer electronics</a>, rather than technology as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Stiegler" title="Bernard Stiegler">Bernard Stiegler</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Technics_and_Time,_1" title="Technics and Time, 1">Technics and Time, 1</a></i>, defines technology in two ways: as "the pursuit of life by means other than life," and as "organized inorganic matter."<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Technology can be most broadly defined as the entities, both material and immaterial, created by the application of mental and physical effort in order to achieve some value. In this usage, technology refers to tools and machines that may be used to solve real-world problems. It is a far-reaching term that may include simple tools, such as a <a href="/wiki/Crowbar_(tool)" title="Crowbar (tool)">crowbar</a> or wooden <a href="/wiki/Spoon" title="Spoon">spoon</a>, or more complex machines, such as a <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> or <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerator</a>. Tools and machines need not be material; <span id="Virtual_technology"></span>virtual technology, such as <a href="/wiki/Computer_software" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer software">computer software</a> and <a href="/wiki/Business_method" class="mw-redirect" title="Business method">business methods</a>, fall under this definition of technology.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/W._Brian_Arthur" title="W. Brian Arthur">W. Brian Arthur</a> defines technology in a similarly broad way as "a means to fulfill a human purpose."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The word "technology" can also be used to refer to a collection of techniques. In this context, it is the current state of humanity's knowledge of how to combine resources to produce desired products, to solve problems, fulfill needs, or satisfy wants; it includes technical methods, skills, processes, techniques, tools and raw materials. When combined with another term, such as "medical technology" or "space technology," it refers to the state of the respective field's knowledge and tools. "<a href="/wiki/State-of-the-art" class="mw-redirect" title="State-of-the-art">State-of-the-art</a> technology" refers to the <a href="/wiki/High_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="High technology">high technology</a> available to humanity in any field.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:C4004_(Intel).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/C4004_%28Intel%29.jpg/220px-C4004_%28Intel%29.jpg" width="220" height="75" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/C4004_%28Intel%29.jpg/330px-C4004_%28Intel%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/C4004_%28Intel%29.jpg/440px-C4004_%28Intel%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="208" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:C4004_(Intel).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The invention of integrated circuits and the <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessor</a> (here, an <a href="/wiki/Intel_4004" title="Intel 4004">Intel 4004</a> chip from 1971) led to the modern <a href="/wiki/Computer_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer revolution">computer revolution</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Technology can be viewed as an activity that forms or changes culture.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Additionally, technology is the application of math, science, and the arts for the benefit of life as it is known. A modern example is the rise of <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> technology, which has lessened barriers to human interaction and as a result has helped spawn new subcultures; the rise of <a href="/wiki/Cyberculture" title="Cyberculture">cyberculture</a> has at its basis the development of the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Not all technology enhances culture in a creative way; technology can also help facilitate <a href="/wiki/Political_oppression" class="mw-redirect" title="Political oppression">political oppression</a> and war via tools such as guns. As a cultural activity, technology predates both <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, each of which formalize some aspects of technological endeavor.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science.2C_engineering_and_technology">Science, engineering and technology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg/220px-Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg/330px-Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg/440px-Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="430" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zoom_lunette_ardente.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> conducting an experiment with combustion generated by amplified sun light</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The distinction between science, engineering, and technology is not always clear. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Science</a> is systematic knowledge of the physical or material world gained through observation and experimentation.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Technologies are not usually exclusively products of science, because they have to satisfy requirements such as <a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Usability" title="Usability">usability</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Safety" title="Safety">safety</a>.</p>
<p>Engineering is the <a href="/wiki/Goal-oriented" class="mw-redirect" title="Goal-oriented">goal-oriented</a> process of designing and making tools and systems to exploit natural phenomena for practical human means, often (but not always) using results and techniques from science. The development of technology may draw upon many fields of knowledge, including scientific, engineering, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">linguistic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">historical</a> knowledge, to achieve some practical result.</p>
<p>Technology is often a consequence of science and engineering, although technology as a human activity precedes the two fields. For example, science might study the flow of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductors</a> by using already-existing tools and knowledge. This new-found knowledge may then be used by engineers to create new tools and machines such as <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computers</a>, and other forms of advanced technology. In this sense, scientists and engineers may both be considered technologists; the three fields are often considered as one for the purposes of research and reference.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The exact relations between science and technology in particular have been debated by scientists, historians, and policymakers in the late 20th century, in part because the debate can inform the funding of basic and applied science. In the immediate wake of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, for example, it was widely considered in the United States that technology was simply "applied science" and that to fund basic science was to reap technological results in due time. An articulation of this philosophy could be found explicitly in <a href="/wiki/Vannevar_Bush" title="Vannevar Bush">Vannevar Bush</a>'s treatise on postwar science policy, <i>Science – The Endless Frontier</i>: "New products, new industries, and more jobs require continuous additions to knowledge of the laws of nature&#160;... This essential new knowledge can be obtained only through basic scientific research."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In the late-1960s, however, this view came under direct attack, leading towards initiatives to fund science for specific tasks (initiatives resisted by the scientific community). The issue remains contentious, though most analysts resist the model that technology simply is a result of scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_technology" title="History of technology">History of technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_historic_inventions" title="Timeline of historic inventions">Timeline of historic inventions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_electrical_and_electronic_engineering" title="Timeline of electrical and electronic engineering">Timeline of electrical and electronic engineering</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleolithic_.282.5_Ma.C2.A0.E2.80.93_10_ka.29">Paleolithic (2.5 Ma&#160;– 10 ka)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png/220px-Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png/330px-Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png/440px-Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png 2x" data-file-width="728" data-file-height="324" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chopper_of_Dmanisi.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A primitive <a href="/wiki/Chopper_(archaeology)" title="Chopper (archaeology)">chopper</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_prehistoric_technology" title="Outline of prehistoric technology">Outline of prehistoric technology</a></div>
<p>The use of tools by <a href="/wiki/Homo_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo (genus)">early humans</a> was partly a process of discovery and of evolution. Early humans evolved from a <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">foraging</a> <a href="/wiki/Hominids" class="mw-redirect" title="Hominids">hominids</a> which were already <a href="/wiki/Bipedal" class="mw-redirect" title="Bipedal">bipedal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> with a brain mass approximately one third of modern humans.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Tool use remained relatively unchanged for most of early human history. Approximately 50,000 years ago, the use of tools and <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">complex set of behaviors</a> emerged, believed by many archaeologists to be connected to the emergence of fully modern <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stone_tools">Stone tools</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg/170px-Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg/255px-Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg/340px-Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4061" data-file-height="4060" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biface_de_St_Acheul_MHNT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hand axes from the <a href="/wiki/Acheulian" class="mw-redirect" title="Acheulian">Acheulian</a> period</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clovis_Point.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Clovis_Point.jpg/170px-Clovis_Point.jpg" width="170" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Clovis_Point.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="178" data-file-height="261" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clovis_Point.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Clovis_point" title="Clovis point">Clovis point</a>, made via <a href="/wiki/Lithic_reduction#Pressure_flaking" title="Lithic reduction">pressure flaking</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hominids started using primitive stone tools millions of years ago. The earliest stone tools were little more than a fractured rock, but approximately 75,000 years ago,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lithic_reduction#Pressure_flaking" title="Lithic reduction">pressure flaking</a> provided a way to make much finer work.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire">Fire</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">Control of fire by early humans</a></div>
<p>The discovery and utilization of <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fire</a>, a simple <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> source with many profound uses, was a turning point in the technological evolution of humankind.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The exact date of its discovery is not known; evidence of burnt animal bones at the <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_Humankind" title="Cradle of Humankind">Cradle of Humankind</a> suggests that the domestication of fire occurred before 1 Ma;<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> scholarly consensus indicates that <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> had controlled fire by between 500 and 400 ka.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Fire, fueled with <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, allowed early humans to cook their food to increase its digestibility, improving its nutrient value and broadening the number of foods that could be eaten.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing_and_shelter">Clothing and shelter</span></h4>
<p>Other technological advances made during the Paleolithic era were <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a> and shelter; the adoption of both technologies cannot be dated exactly, but they were a key to humanity's progress. As the Paleolithic era progressed, dwellings became more sophisticated and more elaborate; as early as 380 ka, humans were constructing temporary wood huts.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Clothing, adapted from the fur and hides of hunted animals, helped humanity expand into colder regions; humans began to <a href="/wiki/Historical_migration" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical migration">migrate</a> out of Africa by 200 ka and into other continents such as <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic_through_classical_antiquity_.2810_ka.C2.A0.E2.80.93_300_CE.29">Neolithic through classical antiquity (10 ka&#160;– 300 CE)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/220px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/330px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/440px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An array of Neolithic artifacts, including bracelets, axe heads, chisels, and polishing tools</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Man's technological ascent began in earnest in what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> Period ("New Stone Age"). The invention of polished <a href="/wiki/Stone_axe" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone axe">stone axes</a> was a major advance that allowed <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">forest clearance</a> on a large scale to create farms. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> fed larger populations, and the transition to <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentism</a> allowed simultaneously raising more children, as infants no longer needed to be carried, as <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> ones must. Additionally, children could contribute labor to the raising of crops more readily than they could to the <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> economy.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>With this increase in population and availability of labor came an increase in <a href="/wiki/Labor_specialization" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor specialization">labor specialization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> What triggered the progression from early Neolithic villages to the first cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a>, and the first civilizations, such as <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, is not specifically known; however, the emergence of increasingly <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchical</a> social structures and specialized labor, of trade and war amongst adjacent cultures, and the need for collective action to overcome environmental challenges such as <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>, are all thought to have played a role.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_tools">Metal tools</span></h4>
<p>Continuing improvements led to the <a href="/wiki/Furnace" title="Furnace">furnace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bellows" title="Bellows">bellows</a> and provided the ability to <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forge" title="Forge">forge</a> native metals (naturally occurring in relatively pure form).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">Gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, were such early metals. The advantages of copper tools over stone, bone, and wooden tools were quickly apparent to early humans, and native copper was probably used from near the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> times (about 10 ka).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Native copper does not naturally occur in large amounts, but copper ores are quite common and some of them produce metal easily when burned in wood or charcoal fires. Eventually, the working of metals led to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Alloys" class="mw-redirect" title="Alloys">alloys</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a> (about 4000 BCE). The first uses of iron alloys such as <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> dates to around 1800 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_and_transport">Energy and transport</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wheel_Iran.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wheel_Iran.jpg/170px-Wheel_Iran.jpg" width="170" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wheel_Iran.jpg/255px-Wheel_Iran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/Wheel_Iran.jpg 2x" data-file-width="275" data-file-height="305" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wheel_Iran.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a> was invented circa 4000 BCE.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_transport" title="History of transport">History of transport</a></div>
<p>Meanwhile, humans were learning to harness other forms of energy. The earliest known use of wind power is the sailboat; the earliest record of a ship under sail is that of a Nile boat that dates back to the 8th millennium BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> From prehistoric times, Egyptians probably used the power of the annual <a href="/wiki/Flooding_of_the_Nile" title="Flooding of the Nile">flooding of the Nile</a> to irrigate their lands, gradually learning to regulate much of it through purposely built irrigation channels and "catch" basins. Similarly, the early peoples of Mesopotamia, the Sumerians, learned to use the Tigris and Euphrates Rivers for much the same purposes. However, more extensive use of wind and water (and even human) power required another invention.</p>
<p>According to archaeologists, the <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a> was invented around 4000 BCE probably independently and nearly simultaneously in Mesopotamia (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>), the Northern Caucasus (<a href="/wiki/Maykop_culture" title="Maykop culture">Maykop culture</a>) and Central Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Estimates on when this may have occurred range from 5500 to 3000 BCE with most experts putting it closer to 4000 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The oldest artifacts with drawings that depict wheeled carts date from about 3500 BCE;<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> however, the wheel may have been in use for millennia before these drawings were made. There is also evidence from the same period for the use of the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">potter's wheel</a>. More recently, the oldest-known wooden wheel in the world was found in the Ljubljana marshes of Slovenia.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The invention of the wheel revolutionized trade and war. It did not take long to discover that wheeled wagons could be used to carry heavy loads. Fast (rotary) potters' wheels enabled early <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a> of pottery, but it was the use of the wheel as a transformer of energy (through <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheels</a>, windmills, and even treadmills) that revolutionized the application of nonhuman power sources.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_and_modern_history_.28300_CE.C2.A0.E2.80.93_present.29">Medieval and modern history (300 CE&#160;– present)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_technology" title="Medieval technology">Medieval technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_technology" title="Renaissance technology">Renaissance technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">Second Industrial Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_Technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Information Technology">Information Technology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Productivity_improving_technologies_(economic_history)" class="mw-redirect" title="Productivity improving technologies (economic history)">Productivity improving technologies (economic history)</a></div>
<p>Innovations continued through the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> with innovations such as <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Horse_collar" title="Horse collar">horse collar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe" title="Horseshoe">horseshoes</a> in the first few hundred years after the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Medieval_technology" title="Medieval technology">Medieval technology</a> saw the use of <a href="/wiki/Simple_machine" title="Simple machine">simple machines</a> (such as the <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">lever</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Screw" title="Screw">screw</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pulley" title="Pulley">pulley</a>) being combined to form more complicated tools, such as the <a href="/wiki/Wheelbarrow" title="Wheelbarrow">wheelbarrow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clock" title="Clock">clocks</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_technology" title="Renaissance technology">Renaissance</a> brought forth many of these innovations, including the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> (which facilitated the greater communication of knowledge), and technology became increasingly associated with <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, beginning a cycle of mutual advancement. The advancements in technology in this era allowed a more steady supply of food, followed by the wider availability of consumer goods.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg/180px-Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg" width="180" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg/270px-Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg/360px-Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="365" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Late_model_Ford_Model_T.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile">automobile</a> revolutionized personal transportation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Starting in the United Kingdom in the 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> was a period of great technological discovery, particularly in the areas of <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transport</a>, driven by the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Steam_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam power">steam power</a>. Technology took another step in a <a href="/wiki/Second_industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Second industrial revolution">second industrial revolution</a> with the harnessing of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> to create such innovations as the <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Light_bulb" class="mw-redirect" title="Light bulb">light bulb</a>, and countless others. Scientific advancement and the discovery of new concepts later allowed for <a href="/wiki/Flight" title="Flight">powered flight</a> and advancements in <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>. The rise in technology has led to <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscrapers</a> and broad <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban areas</a> whose inhabitants rely on <a href="/wiki/Motor" class="mw-redirect" title="Motor">motors</a> to transport them and their food supply. Communication was also greatly improved with the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telephone" title="Telephone">telephone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>. The late 19th and early 20th centuries saw a revolution in transportation with the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile">automobile</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg/220px-USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg/330px-USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg/440px-USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1274" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USAF_F-16A_F-15C_F-15E_Desert_Storm_edit2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/F-15" class="mw-redirect" title="F-15">F-15</a> and <a href="/wiki/F-16" class="mw-redirect" title="F-16">F-16</a> flying over <a href="/wiki/Kuwaiti_oil_fires" title="Kuwaiti oil fires">Kuwaiti oil fires</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> in 1991.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 20th century brought a host of innovations. In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> has led to both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>. <a href="/wiki/Computers" class="mw-redirect" title="Computers">Computers</a> were also invented and later <a href="/wiki/Miniaturization" title="Miniaturization">miniaturized</a> utilizing <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuits</a>. <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">Information technology</a> subsequently led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, which ushered in the current <a href="/wiki/Information_Age" title="Information Age">Information Age</a>. Humans have also been able to <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">explore space</a> with <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a> (later used for <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">telecommunication</a>) and in manned missions going all the way to the moon. In medicine, this era brought innovations such as <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_surgery" title="Cardiac surgery">open-heart surgery</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell_treatments" class="mw-redirect" title="Stem cell treatments">stem cell therapy</a> along with new <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" title="Pharmaceutical drug">medications</a> and treatments.</p>
<p>Complex <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a> techniques and organizations are needed to make and maintain these new technologies, and entire <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industries</a> have arisen to support and develop succeeding generations of increasingly more complex tools. Modern technology increasingly relies on training and education&#160;– their designers, builders, maintainers, and users often require sophisticated general and specific training. Moreover, these technologies have become so complex that entire fields have been created to support them, including <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, and other fields have been made more complex, such as <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Transportation">transportation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technicism">Technicism</span></h3>
<p>Generally, <a href="/wiki/Technicism" title="Technicism">technicism</a> is the belief in the utility of technology for improving human societies.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Taken to an extreme, technicism "reflects a fundamental attitude which seeks to control reality, to resolve all problems with the use of scientific-technological methods and tools."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In other words, human beings will someday be able to master all problems and possibly even control the future using technology. Some, such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stephen_V._Monsma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stephen V. Monsma (page does not exist)">Stephen V. Monsma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Monsma_1986_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monsma_1986-48">[48]</a></sup> connect these ideas to the abdication of religion as a higher <a href="/wiki/Moral_authority" title="Moral authority">moral authority</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optimism">Optimism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Extropianism" title="Extropianism">Extropianism</a></div>
<p>Optimistic assumptions are made by proponents of ideologies such as <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singularitarianism" title="Singularitarianism">singularitarianism</a>, which view <a href="/wiki/Technological_evolution" title="Technological evolution">technological development</a> as generally having beneficial effects for the society and the human condition. In these ideologies, technological development is morally good.</p>
<p>Transhumanists generally believe that the point of technology is to overcome barriers, and that what we commonly refer to as the <a href="/wiki/Human_condition" title="Human condition">human condition</a> is just another barrier to be surpassed.</p>
<p>Singularitarians believe in some sort of "<a href="/wiki/Accelerating_change" title="Accelerating change">accelerating change</a>"; that the rate of technological progress accelerates as we obtain more technology, and that this will culminate in a "<a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">Singularity</a>" after <a href="/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence" title="Artificial general intelligence">artificial general intelligence</a> is invented in which progress is nearly infinite; hence the term. Estimates for the date of this Singularity vary,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> but prominent futurist <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> estimates the Singularity will occur in 2045.</p>
<p>Kurzweil is also known for his history of the universe in six epochs: (1) the physical/chemical epoch, (2) the life epoch, (3) the human/brain epoch, (4) the technology epoch, (5) the artificial intelligence epoch, and (6) the universal colonization epoch. Going from one epoch to the next is a Singularity in its own right, and a period of speeding up precedes it. Each epoch takes a shorter time, which means the whole history of the universe is one giant Singularity event.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some critics see these ideologies as examples of <a href="/wiki/Scientism" title="Scientism">scientism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Techno-utopianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Techno-utopianism">techno-utopianism</a> and fear the notion of <a href="/wiki/Human_enhancement" title="Human enhancement">human enhancement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">technological singularity</a> which they support. Some have described <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> as a techno-optimist.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skepticism_and_critics">Skepticism and critics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Luddite" title="Luddite">Luddite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-Luddism" title="Neo-Luddism">Neo-Luddism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-primitivism" title="Anarcho-primitivism">Anarcho-primitivism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bioconservatism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioconservatism">Bioconservatism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Refer to caption" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/220px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/330px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/440px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="602" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Luddites" class="mw-redirect" title="Luddites">Luddites</a> smashing a power loom in 1812</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the somewhat skeptical side are certain philosophers like <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Marcuse" title="Herbert Marcuse">Herbert Marcuse</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Zerzan" title="John Zerzan">John Zerzan</a>, who believe that technological societies are inherently flawed. They suggest that the inevitable result of such a society is to become evermore technological at the cost of freedom and psychological health.</p>
<p>Many, such as the <a href="/wiki/Luddites" class="mw-redirect" title="Luddites">Luddites</a> and prominent philosopher <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a>, hold serious, although not entirely, deterministic reservations about technology (see "<a href="/wiki/The_Question_Concerning_Technology" title="The Question Concerning Technology">The Question Concerning Technology</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-Heidegger_1977_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heidegger_1977-52">[52]</a></sup>). According to Heidegger scholars <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus" title="Hubert Dreyfus">Hubert Dreyfus</a> and Charles Spinosa, "Heidegger does not oppose technology. He hopes to reveal the essence of technology in a way that 'in no way confines us to a stultified compulsion to push on blindly with technology or, what comes to the same thing, to rebel helplessly against it.' Indeed, he promises that 'when we once open ourselves expressly to the essence of technology, we find ourselves unexpectedly taken into a freeing claim.'<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> What this entails is a more complex relationship to technology than either techno-optimists or techno-pessimists tend to allow."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the most poignant criticisms of technology are found in what are now considered to be dystopian literary classics such as <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World">Brave New World</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Burgess" title="Anthony Burgess">Anthony Burgess</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Clockwork_Orange_(novel)" title="A Clockwork Orange (novel)">A Clockwork Orange</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four">Nineteen Eighty-Four</a></i>. In <a href="/wiki/Goethe%27s_Faust" title="Goethe's Faust">Goethe's <i>Faust</i></a>, Faust selling his soul to the devil in return for power over the physical world is also often interpreted as a metaphor for the adoption of industrial technology. More recently, modern works of science fiction such as those by <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Gibson" title="William Gibson">William Gibson</a> and films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Blade_Runner" title="Blade Runner">Blade Runner</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ghost_in_the_Shell_(1995_film)" title="Ghost in the Shell (1995 film)">Ghost in the Shell</a></i> project highly ambivalent or cautionary attitudes toward technology's impact on human society and identity.</p>
<p>The late cultural critic <a href="/wiki/Neil_Postman" title="Neil Postman">Neil Postman</a> distinguished tool-using societies from technological societies and from what he called "technopolies," societies that are dominated by the ideology of technological and scientific progress to the exclusion or harm of other cultural practices, values and world-views.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Darin_Barney" title="Darin Barney">Darin Barney</a> has written about technology's impact on practices of <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">citizenship</a> and democratic culture, suggesting that technology can be construed as (1) an object of political debate, (2) a means or medium of discussion, and (3) a setting for democratic deliberation and citizenship. As a setting for democratic culture, Barney suggests that technology tends to make <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> questions, including the question of what a good life consists in, nearly impossible, because they already give an answer to the question: a good life is one that includes the use of more and more technology.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nikolas_Kompridis" title="Nikolas Kompridis">Nikolas Kompridis</a> has also <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.parrhesiajournal.org/parrhesia08/parrhesia08_kompridis.pdf">written</a> about the dangers of new technology, such as <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">synthetic biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>. He warns that these technologies introduce unprecedented new challenges to human beings, including the possibility of the permanent alteration of our biological nature. These concerns are shared by other philosophers, scientists and public intellectuals who have written about similar issues (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Francis_Fukuyama" title="Francis Fukuyama">Francis Fukuyama</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Habermas" title="Jürgen Habermas">Jürgen Habermas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Joy" title="Bill Joy">William Joy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Sandel" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael Sandel">Michael Sandel</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another prominent critic of technology is <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus" title="Hubert Dreyfus">Hubert Dreyfus</a>, who has published books such as <i>On the Internet</i> and <i>What Computers Still Can't Do</i>.</p>
<p>A more infamous anti-technological treatise is <i><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Industrial_Society_and_Its_Future" class="extiw" title="s:Industrial Society and Its Future">Industrial Society and Its Future</a></i>, written by the Unabomber <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kaczynski" title="Ted Kaczynski">Ted Kaczynski</a> and printed in several major newspapers (and later books) as part of an effort to end his bombing campaign of the techno-industrial infrastructure.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appropriate_technology">Appropriate technology</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Technocriticism" title="Technocriticism">Technocriticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technorealism" title="Technorealism">Technorealism</a></div>
<p>The notion of <a href="/wiki/Appropriate_technology" title="Appropriate technology">appropriate technology</a> was developed in the 20th century by thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/E._F._Schumacher" title="E. F. Schumacher">E. F. Schumacher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Ellul" title="Jacques Ellul">Jacques Ellul</a> to describe situations where it was not desirable to use very new technologies or those that required access to some centralized <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> or parts or skills imported from elsewhere. The <a href="/wiki/Ecovillage" title="Ecovillage">ecovillage</a> movement emerged in part due to this concern.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optimism_and_skepticism_in_the_21st_century">Optimism and skepticism in the 21st century</span></h3>
<p><i>This section mainly focuses on American concerns even if it can reasonably be generalized to other Western countries.</i></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The inadequate quantity and quality of American jobs is one of the most fundamental economic challenges we face. [...] What's the linkage between technology and this fundamental problem?</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Bernstein, Jared, "It’s Not a Skills Gap That’s Holding Wages Down: It’s the Weak Economy, Among Other Things," in <a href="/wiki/The_American_Prospect" title="The American Prospect">The American Prospect</a>, October 2014</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In his article, <a href="/wiki/Jared_Bernstein" title="Jared Bernstein">Jared Bernstein</a>, a Senior Fellow at the <a href="/wiki/Center_on_Budget_and_Policy_Priorities" title="Center on Budget and Policy Priorities">Center on Budget and Policy Priorities</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> questions the widespread idea that <a href="/wiki/Automation" title="Automation">automation</a>, and more broadly, technological advances, have mainly contributed to this growing <a href="/wiki/Labor_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor market">labor market</a> problem. His thesis appears to be a third way between optimism and skepticism. Essentially, he stands for a neutral approach of the linkage between technology and American issues concerning <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> and declining wages.</p>
<p>He uses two main arguments to defend his point. First, because of recent technological advances, an increasing number of workers are losing their jobs. Yet, scientific evidence fails to clearly demonstrate that technology has displaced so many workers that it has created more problems than it has solved. Indeed, <a href="/wiki/Automation" title="Automation">automation</a> threatens repetitive jobs but higher-end jobs are still necessary because they complement technology and manual jobs that "requires flexibility judgment and common sense"<sup id="cite_ref-:0_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup> remain hard to replace with <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machines</a>. Second, studies have not shown clear links between recent technology advances and the wage trends of the last decades.</p>
<p>Therefore, according to Bernstein, instead of focusing on technology and its hypothetical influences on current American increasing unemployment and declining wages, one needs to worry more about "bad policy that fails to offset the imbalances in demand, trade, income and opportunity."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_technological_systems">Complex technological systems</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_P._Hughes" title="Thomas P. Hughes">Thomas P. Hughes</a> stated that because technology has been considered as a key way to solve problems, we need to be aware of its complex and varied characters to use it more efficiently.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-60">[60]</a></sup> What is the difference between a <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a> and cooking machines such as an <a href="/wiki/Oven" title="Oven">oven</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Gas_stove" title="Gas stove">gas stove</a>? Can we consider all of them, only a part of them, or none of them as technologies?</p>
<p>Technology is often considered too narrowly; according to Hughes, "Technology is a creative process involving human ingenuity.<sup id="cite_ref-Hughes.2C_Thomas_P._2004_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes.2C_Thomas_P._2004-61">[61]</a></sup> This definition's emphasis on creativity avoids unbounded definitions that may mistakenly include cooking “technologies," but it also highlights the prominent role of humans and therefore their responsibilities for the use of complex technological systems.</p>
<p>Yet, because technology is everywhere and has dramatically changed landscapes and societies, Hughes argues that <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Management" title="Management">managers</a> have often believed that they can use technology to shape the world as they want. They have often supposed that technology is easily controllable and this assumption has to be thoroughly questioned.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-60">[60]</a></sup> For instance, <a href="/wiki/Evgeny_Morozov" title="Evgeny Morozov">Evgeny Morozov</a> particularly challenges two concepts: “Internet-centrism” and “solutionism."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Internet-centrism refers to the idea that our society is convinced that the Internet is one of the most stable and coherent forces. Solutionism is the ideology that every social issue can be solved thanks to technology and especially thanks to the internet. In fact, technology intrinsically contains uncertainties and limitations. According to <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Madrigal" title="Alexis Madrigal">Alexis Madrigal</a>'s review of Morozov's theory, to ignore it will lead to “unexpected consequences that could eventually cause more damage than the problems they seek to address."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Benjamin R. Cohen and Gwen Ottinger also discussed the multivalent effects of technology.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Therefore, recognition of the limitations of technology, and more broadly, scientific knowledge, is needed&#160;– especially in cases dealing with <a href="/wiki/Environmental_justice" title="Environmental justice">environmental justice</a> and health issues. Ottinger continues this reasoning and argues that the ongoing recognition of the limitations of scientific knowledge goes hand in hand with scientists and engineers’ new comprehension of their role. Such an approach of technology and science "[require] technical professionals to conceive of their roles in the process differently. [They have to consider themselves as] collaborators in research and problem solving rather than simply providers of information and technical solutions."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitiveness">Competitiveness</span></h2>
<p>Technology is properly defined as any application of science to accomplish a function. The science can be leading edge or well established and the function can have high visibility or be significantly more mundane, but it is all technology, and its exploitation is the foundation of all competitive advantage.</p>
<p>Technology-based planning is what was used to build the US industrial giants before WWII (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Dow_Chemical_Company" title="Dow Chemical Company">Dow</a>, <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">GM</a>) and it is what was used to transform the US into a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a>. It was not economic-based planning.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_Socrates">Project Socrates</span></h3>
<p>In 1983 <a href="/wiki/Project_Socrates" title="Project Socrates">Project Socrates</a> was initiated in the <a href="/wiki/US_intelligence_community" class="mw-redirect" title="US intelligence community">US intelligence community</a> to determine the source of declining US economic and military competitiveness. Project Socrates concluded that technology exploitation is the foundation of all <a href="/wiki/Competitive_advantage" title="Competitive advantage">competitive advantage</a> and that declining US competitiveness was from decision-making in the private and public sectors switching from technology exploitation (technology-based planning) to money exploitation (economic-based planning) at the end of World War II.</p>
<p>Project Socrates determined that to rebuild US competitiveness, decision making throughout the US had to readopt technology-based planning. Project Socrates also determined that countries like China and India had continued executing technology-based (while the US took its detour into economic-based) planning, and as a result had considerably advanced the process and were using it to build themselves into superpowers. To rebuild US competitiveness the US decision-makers needed to adopt a form of technology-based planning that was far more advanced than that used by China and India.</p>
<p>Project Socrates determined that technology-based planning makes an evolutionary leap forward every few hundred years and the next evolutionary leap, the Automated Innovation Revolution, was poised to occur. In the Automated Innovation Revolution the process for determining how to acquire and utilize technology for a competitive advantage (which includes R&amp;D) is automated so that it can be executed with unprecedented speed, efficiency and agility.</p>
<p>Project Socrates developed the means for automated innovation so that the US could lead the Automated Innovation Revolution in order to rebuild and maintain the country's economic competitiveness for many generations.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animal_species">Other animal species</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">Tool use by animals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structures_built_by_animals" title="Structures built by animals">Structures built by animals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_engineer" title="Ecosystem engineer">Ecosystem engineer</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gorilla_tool_use.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Gorilla_tool_use.png/220px-Gorilla_tool_use.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Gorilla_tool_use.png/330px-Gorilla_tool_use.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Gorilla_tool_use.png/440px-Gorilla_tool_use.png 2x" data-file-width="1370" data-file-height="916" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gorilla_tool_use.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This adult <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorilla</a> uses a branch as a <a href="/wiki/Walking_stick" title="Walking stick">walking stick</a> to gauge the water's depth, an example of technology usage by non-human primates.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The use of basic technology is also a feature of other animal species apart from humans. These include primates such as <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> some <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphin</a> communities,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Considering a more generic perspective of technology as ethology of active environmental conditioning and control, we can also refer to animal examples such as beavers and their dams, or bees and their honeycombs.</p>
<p>The ability to make and use tools was once considered a defining characteristic of the genus <a href="/wiki/Homo_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo (genus)">Homo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> However, the discovery of tool construction among chimpanzees and related primates has discarded the notion of the use of technology as unique to humans. For example, researchers have observed wild chimpanzees utilising tools for foraging: some of the tools used include leaf sponges, termite fishing probes, <a href="/wiki/Pestle" class="mw-redirect" title="Pestle">pestles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">levers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/West_African_chimpanzee" class="mw-redirect" title="West African chimpanzee">West African chimpanzees</a> also use stone hammers and anvils for cracking nuts,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> as do <a href="/wiki/Capuchin_monkey" title="Capuchin monkey">capuchin monkeys</a> of <a href="/wiki/Boa_Vista,_Roraima" title="Boa Vista, Roraima">Boa Vista</a>, Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_technology">Future technology</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Emerging_technologies" title="Emerging technologies">Emerging technologies</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Theories_of_technology" title="Theories of technology">Theories of technology</a> often attempt to predict the future of technology based on the <a href="/wiki/High_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="High technology">high technology</a> and science of the time. As with all predictions of the future, however, technology's is uncertain.</p>
<p>Futurist <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil" title="Ray Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a> predicts that the future of technology will be mainly consist of an overlapping "GNR Revolution" of Genetics, Nanotechnology, and Robotics, with robotics being the most important of the three.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>

The MIT Press. <b>108</b> (3): 681–716. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.2307%2F2118405">10.2307/2118405</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/2118405">2118405</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATechnology&amp;rft.atitle=Population+Growth+and+Technological+Change%3A+One+Million+B.C.+to+1990&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft.aulast=Kremer&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F2118405&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F2118405&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Quarterly+Journal+of+Economics&amp;rft.pages=681-716&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=108" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
